```

P.S. to that last.  Having gone and checked my Fowlers, Chicago Manual
of Style, Canadian Press Handbook, etc., I am informed that the correct
usage in English is as follows:

An acronym, when used as a name, is capitalised normally if spoken as a
word.  If spoken by repeating the individual letters, then all letters are
capitalised.

Thus:   Cobol, Fortran, APL, and PL1.
```

```


 
On Thu, 25 Jun 1998, Donald Tees wrote:

=> >Then where does "C" fit in ?
=> 
=> Right after B?
=> 

No,no.no... it follows the rules you cited.

1.)  Every letter in it is pronounced (just one) so all the letters are 
     capitalized - again, just one.

2.)  It is pronounced like a word ("sea", "see"), so JUST the first letter
     is capitalized and the rest (none) are not capitalized.
    
Besides, its rightful place is just before "OBOL". 

rrg
================================================================
"Not as good as I used to be. Never was."
"Time costs money; even worse, money costs time."
"Y2K problem? Unplug the calendar."
"The big Bob wolfe cast aspersions on my VAX; I shall have to
 collect my Winchesters to drive him away."
===============================================================
```

## Thread: let's pretend — 5 msgs

### let's pretend

- **From:** higgish@dhfs.state.wi.us
- **Date:** 1998-06-23T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6mp2ei$gc2@pdrn.zippo.com>`

```

that i'm an idiot. (no peanut gallery remarks, please!)

i've been commandeered for the dreaded y2k effort here in the great state of
wisconsin, and we have no support for ASM programs, yet we have them.

i am in the process of re-writing these beasties to cobol. the current one i am
struggling with is an 'operand parser'. if there is a full operand, it consists
of:

FF LLLL DD MON YY  (first 2 of first name, first 2 of last name, rest fairly
understandable as is??).

_But_, there may be this whole string, or there may be just the last name and
year,
or last name, day, month, year,
or first, last, year
or first, last, day, month, year.

_And_, there can be place-holders (#) or not, in the instance of a 'shorter than
4 letter' last name. as in, for a last name of 'uy' (an actual co-workers last
name), the operand could look like this:
 'UY##b97' or 'UYbbb97'.

the possible number of permutations of this is somewhat boggling to me, and not
having a good (schooled) grasp of ASM, i'd rather not de-construct this
puppy;besides, i think the ASM is unnecessarily convoluted (or is that a
prerequisite with ASM?) in achieving its goal.

now, if we could just go with how to accomplish this (which my first inclination
is to unstring based on......something which my brain hasn't fully grasped yet),
and not get into any of the potential side issues such as y2k and asm and
not-so-brilliant designers of systems and shops that don't support things and
don't tell the IS staff until....wait, they never did announce it, i had to dig
it out of them.... anyway, is unstring in this instance the way to go? and if i
do an unstring (delimited on spaces) of the whole operand, how do i then go
about determining which piece is in which slot--size won't work, numeric won't
work, order in the slots can't be counted on to be consistent given the various
combinations that are possible/permissible.

or maybe i just need to step back,sleep on it, something.

suzie

------------------
This public news site made possible by
the folks at http://extra.newsguy.com
```

#### ↳ Re: let's pretend

- **From:** "Dave Johnson" <systemad@globalnet.co.uk>
- **Date:** 1998-06-23T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6mpd6j$hrg$1@heliodor.xara.net>`

```

Even with ASM, there must be some structure - the example of 'UYbbb97'
doesn't tie in - if it's 'bUYbbb97' you could parse the string taking a
space
as 'end of word' or 'no word' I suppose - I can drop a 'get next word'
routine
if you need a laugh (sounds like you might).

From what you say, the unstring may not be the best answer (as it looks
like a space may be terminating a 'word' or instead of a word). If you are
on VS/2, reference modification is the way (or redefine the input as PIC X
occurs n). Splitting the input into several words may be the start of your
problems as you then have a bit of combination checking - eg if you get
numeric/alpha/numeric, it must be a date - unfortunately alpha/numeric
could be anything (forename DD, forename YY, surname....... etc).

Working backwards, YY should be fairly easy (until 2 years time), and if
MON is really 'mon' (JAN, FEB, MAR etc), again Ok until MAY, which could
be a surname, or DEC which could be a forename (if you are a member of
that popular beat combo 'The Batchelors').

Sounds like you may have to dig backwards and find the users (then shoot
them... only joking, honest). This may be generated by another program or
script which you can check/change or may at least give you some clues.
In the spirit of wild optimism, it wouldn't be first time I'd be told 'oh
that, we
never use it 'cause it doesn't work' (you can always hope).

Anyway, hope that's a start.
DaveJ.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: let's pretend

- **From:** "Page-Cray" <page-cray@email.msn.com>
- **Date:** 1998-06-24T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<#oCG1myn9GA.260@upnetnews05>`
- **References:** `<6mp2ei$gc2@pdrn.zippo.com> <6mpd6j$hrg$1@heliodor.xara.net>`

```

?? is alpha alpha number alpha number the sequence if so then try this.

doing scan left to right find the first " " (space) separator/delimiter see
note below
do a table test for alpha if it is then you have the first unless first
could be missing
if so then you would have to decide but not until the next field is
determined

do the next scan same left to right till next separator " " (space) or any
non space character except alpha or numeric or hyphen for compound names and
also the apostrophe for O'names as will be your separator/delimiter
do a table test for alpha if it is then you have last other wise you have
numeric or special characters other than alpha. Also a determination needs
to be made that the first scan was alpha and this is numeric. you must
decide what you want to do with this is it first or is it last which one is
missing. If it was numeric then you have DD.

If the prior was alpha and and you have first and last do a scan for the
next and check for alpha again. If it is then the DD is missing and you have
MON (alpha month).
If it is not alpha then you have YY.

do the final scan for the next and check for alpha again. if it is all
spaces then (put a limit on all scans to the size of the record) to
determine that following the MON that you have year.

| ALPHA | ALPHA | NUMERIC | ALPHA | NUMERIC |     A A N A N no problem
| ALPHA | NUMERIC | ALPHA | NUMERIC |                     A N A N     ?? you
decide
| NUMERIC | ALPHA | NUMERIC |                                     N A N
?? you decide
| ALPHA | NUMERIC |
A N             ?? you decide
| NUMERIC |
N                 ?? you decide
| ALPHA |
A                 ?? you decide

since it is based upon positional value of alpha or numeric occurring in
sequence  the scan can build a matrix of acceptable sequences and invalid
sequences with A N used as values within this matrix then comparisons for
valid can be performed like:

AANAN = valid
```

#### ↳ Re: let's pretend--solution and thanks

- **From:** higgish@dhfs.state.wi.us
- **Date:** 1998-06-24T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6mr6bb$cls@pdrn.zippo.com>`
- **References:** `<6mp2ei$gc2@pdrn.zippo.com>`

```

higgish@dhfs.state.wi.us wrote:
<snipped myself>

> _But_, there may be this whole string, or there may be just the last name and
> year,
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

the thing that occurred to me about this is: there four possibilities, each with
a different number of 'pieces', and the # place holder can be turned into spaces
with a inspect replacing before an unstring tallying, after which i can edit the
unstrung fields for validity based on the field count.

thanks much (look what happens when one gets some sleep!)
suzie

------------------
This public news site made possible by
the folks at http://extra.newsguy.com
```

#### ↳ Re: let's pretend

- **From:** Tom Morrison <t.morrison@liant.com>
- **Date:** 1998-06-24T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3591234D.7B1052FF@liant.com>`
- **References:** `<6mp2ei$gc2@pdrn.zippo.com>`

```

Susie:

One technique that may be of some help is to MOVE the string you must
decipher into a temporary string, then use an INSPECT...CONVERTING on
the temporary string to derive a 'character type' for each character in
the string.  I put 'character type' in quotes because this is not some
formal COBOL thing but rather a procedural aid to decoding the string.

To take your example, and assuming that:

1. names do not contain numbers (which may or may not be true in
Wisconsin  :)  );

2. names may contain '#' (which might actually be a placeholder for an
embedded space in the last name, not just a nuisance placeholder for
trailing spaces; e.g. "van#der#Floogl"); and

3. actual space(s) are delimiters between fields,

then do something like this:

01  the-operand   pic x(50).
01  char-type     pic x(51).
* TRICK!! length difference guarantees a SPACE 
* in the last position of char-type
01  operand-count        PIC 99.
01  operand-array.  02 occurs 5 indexed by operand-index.
    03  operand-value pic x(50).
    03  operand-position pic 99.
    03  operand-length pic 99.
    03  operand-mask  pic x(50).
    03  operand-type pic X.
        88  operand-is-not-present value "N".
        88  operand-is-alpha value "A".
        88  operand-is-mixed value "M".
        88  operand-is-numeric value "9".
01  the-operand-length   PIC 99.
01  operand-count        PIC 99.
01  beginning-position   PIC 99.
01  op-length            PIC 99.
01  i                    PIC 99.
01  op-type              PIC X.

[please imagine all other WS definitions the procedural example needs.]

  MOVE 50 TO the-operand-length.
....
  MOVE the-operand TO char-type.
  INSPECT char-type CONVERTING
       "#ABCDEFGHIJKLMHOPQRSTUVWXYZabcdefghijklmonopqrstuvwxyz"
       TO ALL "A".
  INSPECT char-type CONVERTING "0123456789" TO ALL "9".
* at this point char-type identifies the character types of all
* the characters in the-operand.
  MOVE 0 TO operand-count.
  MOVE 1 TO beginning-position.
  INSPECT char-type (beginning-position:)
          TALLYING beginning-position FOR LEADING SPACES
  PERFORM VARYING operand-index FROM 1 BY 1 
            UNTIL operand-index > 5
      SET operand-is-not-present TO TRUE
      IF beginning-position NOT > the-operand-length
          MOVE beginning-position TO operand-position (operand-index)
          MOVE 0 to op-length
          MOVE char-type (beginning-position: 1) TO op-type
* TRICK!! described above used here
          INSPECT char-type (beginning-position:)
              TALLYING op-length FOR CHARACTERS BEFORE INITIAL SPACE
          MOVE 0 TO i
          MOVE op-type TO operand-type (operand-index)
          INSPECT char-type (beginning-position: op-length)
              TALLYING i FOR ALL op-type
          IF i NOT = op-length
              SET operand-is-mixed TO TRUE
          END-IF
          MOVE op-length TO operand-length (operand-index)
          MOVE char-type (beginning-position: op-length)
              TO operand-mask (operand-index)
          MOVE the-operand (beginning-position: op-length)
              TO operand-value (operand-index)
          INSPECT operand-value (operand-index)
              REPLACING ALL "#" BY SPACE
          ADD 1 TO operand-count
          INSPECT char-type (beginning-position:)
              TALLYING beginning-position FOR LEADING SPACES
      END-IF
  END-PERFORM.

At this point, you have a much more manageable array of operands, you
know the operand count and type of each operand, you have a character
type mask for handling mixed alpha and numeric strings (if needed), and
those pesky "#" are already replaced with spaces.

Note:  this routine is offered where-is, as-is, since it hasn't been
compiled much less executed (but I do this kind of thing from time to
time).  You may be required to debug it.

I hope this has helped.

Best regards,
```

```
On Tue, 23 Jun 1998, Judson McClendon wrote:

> Ibis redibis numquam peribis wrote in message ...
> >
…[40 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

This may be one more instance where I am 99% certain and 100% wrong, but
let me clarify my position:

                 a.) the examples I cited started as acronyms at first
                     and wound up as words because of popular usage,
                     even laser and maser. Why not "COBOL"? Unless
                     we are to conclude the COBOL will never be 
                     popular enough???? If so, you are right.  

                 b.) My point about using periods in acronyms is
                     precisely this: even the rules governing how
                     we use acronyms change over time. Why not the
                     rule that we always capitalize all the letters?
                     If the latter is immutable, you are right.
                     

                 c.) I concede I was not clear in my point about NASA,
                     IBM, et al. Because these acronyms refer to unique
                     things, this fact suggests that these acronyms will
                     not become commonly accepted as words. In this case
                     you are correct and it will always be COBOL. In a
                     way NASA, FBI, ... are both acronyms and proper
                     names. However, even some trademarks become commonly
                     accepted as words (Kleenex, Frigidaire, Xerox) and
                     lose their capital letters.

The first lexical reference I checked agrees with you; obviously it is not
a good reference! However, it did leave me with a possible out: after
giving an attribution going back through German and Dutch, it ends by
admitting that the derivation was uncertain due to lack of authoritative
sources. My claim was based on my remembering (chancy thing to do) a
book on the Puritans that I read long ago.

All-in-all your position is looking stronger and mine is looking weaker. 

rrg

"Time costs money; even worse, money costs time."
```

- **From:** EdStevens1@csi.com (Ed Stevens)

#### ↳ Re: CA-EZtrieve

- **From:** mark0young@aol.com (Mark0Young)
- **Date:** 1998-06-24T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1998062405215800.BAA18538@ladder03.news.aol.com>`
- **References:** `<35907705.FDAE9A74@bcpl.net>`

```

In article <35907705.FDAE9A74@bcpl.net>, Steve Young <syoung@bcpl.net> writes:

>Does anyone out there know EZtrieve real well? If so, please post a
>reply. I will post a few questions tomorrow.

Are you referring to the old Easytrieve (EXEC PGM=EASYTREV), which still runs
but is quite old (sometimes called "Easytrieve Classic" by the Computer
Associates technicians), or are you referring to Easytrieve-Plus (EXEC
PGM=EZTPA00), which is now the current product and now includes the ability to
get the 4-digit year of the current system date (SYSDATE-LONG)?

I have forgotten a lot of the old Easytrieve, but I am quite familiar with
Easytrieve-Plus 6.1 as implemented in VSE/ESA.

Mark A. Young
```

## Thread: Y2K:Cobol->C++ — 5 msgs

_Topics: [`Y2K and Year-2000 remediation`](topics.md#y2k) · [`Migration and conversion`](topics.md#migration)_

### Re: Y2K:Cobol->C++

- **From:** scm@enterprise.net (Shaun C. Murray)
- **Date:** 1998-06-23T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6moid7$pf7$3@news.enterprise.net>`
- **References:** `<6mdop8$b2h$1@news.nuri.net> <6mgea9$i81$1@news.igs.net> <6mhga1$ld8$2@heliodor.xara.net>`

```

In article <6mhga1$ld8$2@heliodor.xara.net>, systemad@globalnet.co.uk says...
>
>Mind you, if we are talking PCs, Pascal (Delphi) is a pretty good
>choice (well, it's a b******y excellent choice really, but when did
>technology win over marketing on the PC?)

I don't want to start a language war but...

Most of the new 'business' applications I'm doing these days are in Delphi. 
Whereas I think it's a very good technology for GUI work and interfacing with 
SQL, I find it a royal pain in the ass to maintain other peoples code and the 
performance of typical applications is knowhere near that of COBOL (Micro 
Focus) in my experience. The overheads of file handling via SQL are largely to 
blame. In COBOL I use my own custom non-sql database handler. Raw file 
handling is just so much quicker in COBOL. 

Delphi's threading sucks too. MF COBOL's knocks seven bells out of it.

Where it does score well is in it's framework paradigm. I can re-use a lot of 
code very easily. I have an asset retrevial system with some 1500+ assets to 
call on, most of which are Delphi. I think I put one COBOL asset in there!  OO 
COBOL is not as good and few people understand or use it although I must say 
it's a much fuller OO language than Delphi. Why Borland stuck with Pascal 
instead of Modula (2/3) or Oberon is somewhat puzzling.

Delphi is also superb at prototyping GUI interfaces and doesn't have arcane 
languages behind the app like MF's Dialog system. Mix OO COBOL with Delphi's 
GUI creation ability and I'd be more than happy.


Shaun
```

#### ↳ Re: Y2K:Cobol->C++

- **From:** "Dave Johnson" <systemad@globalnet.co.uk>
- **Date:** 1998-06-23T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6mp5rc$872$2@heliodor.xara.net>`

```

No war taken - the usual problems though. Maintaining other peoples code
can be a pain in any language (depends on the people) - to date, I've only
maintained my own Delphi code, but heaps of 'interesting' COBOL code
(I'm sure on another ng on another planet, someone is saying something
similar about my code...). And yes, it is the SQL that slows things down -
I tend to stick with Paradox.

I do find Delphi more fun, but that may be the type of projects I use it for
(most of my corn comes from mainframe COBOL), so my normal rule is
'enterprise systems in COBOL, anything else in Delphi'. Both languages
are fast, quite easy to maintain, and have as many facilities as you need
(but will never have as many as you want!).

DaveJ.
```

```
Sure, I would agree with you - if it cost $0.00 to maintain the code 
in other languages.

Fact - Cobol is easier to maintan than C
Fact - Cobol code for BUSINESS PURPOSES actually contains less lines 
of code than C

I found out in a big hurry -

Fact - C programmers don't work for free

I beleive it would actually cost MORE to maintain the system written 
in C than it did in COBOL.

PS. I am not attacking C, just using it as an example.   Substitute 
JAVA, PERL, or any of the other fad languages.
```

```
And this is assuming that you can maintain the C code at all:)

I've even seen COBOL that was deliberately written so no one would mess with it
that was still maintainable....and C code that the author couldn't figure out
less than a year later.

And not only can COBOL take fewer lines of code....some COBOL compilers
actually generate a lot tighter machine language than some C compilers.
```

#### ↳ Re: history of cobol

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc123@bellsouth.net>
- **Date:** 1998-06-23T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<_xWj1.438$ei3.1088523@news4.atl.bellsouth.net>`
- **References:** `<358DD9B7.74B5@infoave.net> <6mm1ht$klj$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com> <Sqxj1.66$ei3.213015@news4.atl.bellsouth.net> <6momh8$uqk$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com>`

```

Actually, whether you realize it or not, I was paying you a compliment.
Either your message was a clever troll (meaning you didn't really believe
what you were saying, you were just posting it to get a rise out of us),
or you are abysmally ignorant of the facts.  Not thinking anyone could be
quite so ignorant of the facts, I assumed your message was a clever troll.
Now you disillusion me by saying you really are that ignorant of the
facts, and not really clever enough to have written that message as a
troll.  I hereby publicly apoligize, and retract my compliment.  Does that
make you feel better?

Now, since I must retract my compliment of your post as a clever troll,
I will comment on a few of your serious misconceptions.  I have copied
your original post below for comment.

>> amark@luckynet.co.il wrote:
>> >
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>> >should it be) data baze managing system.

This is a truly amazing statement, and was the cause of my first thoughts
that your post was a troll.  DBMS (DataBase Managament Systems) were first
invented years after COBOL was born.  COBOL is an acronym for COmmon Business
Oriented Language.  That's a real big clue what COBOL was created for.  When
COBOL was created, the punched card was king.  There were no 'DBMS' systems
until many years later.

>> > From cobol steams most of our troubles with 2000 year problemm.

Another truly amazing statement, founded securely in ignorance.  Every single
medical problem involving birth is associated with women.  Does that mean
that women CAUSE them?  Geesh!  There is one, and only one, reason why COBOL
is associated so closely with Y2k problems.  Listen closely now.  It is because
there are no 30 year old C programs, no 30 year old VB programs, no 30 year old
Java programs.  Are you beginning to get the picture?  Tell me which features
in any of these other languages would have prevented a programmer using YYMMDD
for a date in a database, hum?  Now, tell me what feature in COBOL would force
a programmer to use PIC 99 for year, rather than PIC 9999, hum?  Just trot
them out right here and prove me wrong.  The fact is that the Y2k problem has
*NOTHING* do do with the language used.  Unlike proprietary database systems
such as Access, which actually have specific internal date formats, all general
purpose programming languages, such as COBOL, C, Pascal, FORTRAN, you-name-it, allow you to set up a date in any form
you like.  This is fact, friend.  Any
programmer beyond a complete neophyte knows this.  Now, if we took your idea
and converted our 200GB absolutely mission critical databases into Access (an
amusing proposition at best), we would still be hostage to Microsoft's
proprietary date format.

>> >Some 15 years ago they notised, all of a sudden, that in commercial
>> >paccages, writen in cobol there are only two digits in the "year" field.

Another statement from ignorance.  Do you know what percentage of COBOL programs
are in 'commercial packages'?  Probably less than 5%, likely less than 2%. The
*vast* majority of COBOL programs are written as proprietary, custom programs by
and for individual companies or organizations.

And so we 'suddenly noticed' the problem 15 years ago, hum?  What were you doing
15 years ago?  How about 20 years ago?  How about 25 years ago?  How about 30
years ago?  Well, I was writing COBOL code 5, 10, 15, 20, 25 and 30 years ago
(as well as today).  I have written several million lines of COBOL code.  And
I can tell you as an absolute fact that I never wrote a program using a 2 digit
year in my life when I was not aware that there would be a problem with it at
the turn of the century.

I have written many, many programs which used two digit year, but none of them
within the last 15 years.  There are two basic reasons why two digit years were
used in the beginning.  One reason is that resources were extremely limited in
those days.  Memory and storage are so abundant now that no one who wasn't
programming before 1980 can have the foggiest sense of how limited memory and
storage were.  The freedom to simply decide to use 4 digit year was not there.
At every point you had to be concerned with effeciency, efficiency, efficiency.
This attitude younger programmers have now that it doesn't really matter, would
have been ludicrous in those days, and anyone who programmed like that would
have been out of a job in very short order.  The other reason two digit years
were used in the beginning is because nobody, and I mean nobody, really thought,
or had reason to think, that those programs would still be in production 30 or
more years later.  Anyone who says they knew this in the 60's has either
forgotten or is lying.  In 1980, the modern computer industry was only about 20
years old, and had seen enormous change.  Who knew that COBOL would be such a
stable environment that COBOL programs written in 1968 would still compile and
run in 1998?  Can you compile a 25 year old C program on todays compilers?  You
can't even compile a VB 3 program on VB5 without changes, and VB is a baby
compared to COBOL!   You think *unstability* is an advantage?  How much money
do you think has been spent recoding VB1, VB2, VB3 and VB4 code to work with
later versions of VB?  What if all that COBOL code had been written in VB?
You would have a Y2k level effort every two years, just to keep up!  Why do you
think businesses still use COBOL so much?  You think all business programmers
are idiots, and you have the only vision?  It is because COBOL offers features
business find valuable.  But ignorant clucks who have not written hundreds or
thousands of business programs don't have a clue what COBOL is all about.  I
have written production code in over computer 40 languages, and am certainly
not limited to COBOL.  I would not write a text editor or a compiler in COBOL
(though I have), unless I was constrained by other reasons (I was).  But if I
write a core business app like payroll, G/L, etc., you can bet I will write it
in COBOL, unless constrined by other reasons.

By 1985, we knew we were writing COBOL systems which would still be in use by
the year 2000, because, unlike 1975, we already had 20 year old COBOL programs.
Any programs written after 1985, COBOL or otherwise, should have been written
Y2k compliant.  I was certainly preaching that to my clients.  But the decision
not to remediate older system for Y2k was a, listen carefully now, *management*
decision.  Programmers, even COBOL programmers, can recommend a course of
action, but no COBOL programmer I know has authority to allocate thousands or
millions of dollars of company resources to do Y2k remediation.  And you know
what?  Most of those managers who made the decisions to delay or forgo Y2k
remediation probably never saw a line of COBOL in their lives.  So when you say
that the decision had to do with COBOL, you are again speaking from obvious
ignorance.  It had to do with *money*, not programming language.  You think
management would have been more willing to to spend $100 million rewriting
applications in C, than they would have been to spend $20 million for Y2k
remediation of existing COBOL applications?  Do you know how long some of these
systems have been under development and improvement?  You think you could just
whip in there and rewrite a hundred billion lines of COBOL code in C overnight?
Geesh!

As to COBOL being replaced simply because it is old, what kind of eating
instrument do you use?  Chances are, it was designed hundreds, perhaps even
thousands, of years ago.  How many significant improvements on the hammer have
there been in the last century?  If you live long enough, you will hopefully
gain the wisdom that just because something is newer, does not mean it is
better.  Newer might be better, but newer might be worse, too.  Time and
experience reveal this.  Right now, it is more expensive, takes longer, costs
more, and you get a less reliable result, using any language other than COBOL,
for core business programming.  This we learn, if we look at the actual numbers
from real-world projects, rather than blather about in ignorance.
```

```
>Well he still not getting it. In theory, yes, we should never attempt to
>modify the data. That's what the academic point of view. But in true
>life,

Funny, but I have never heard this viewpoint before.  I was always
taught that you fix whatever is wrong.  Fix bad data, bad programs,
bad programmers, just make the damned thing work so we can all
go home and play music or whatever.

I will tell you something that is funny, but embarrassing.  I have my own
Cobol system that I wrote myself to keep my own corporate books. I wrote
that system about ten years ago, and have ported it through two OS's
and four Cobols.  In all that time, I have never gotten around to writing
a year end for it.  I dump the Isam files to flat ASCII, and do the
year end with EDIT.COM.  I then reload them. It takes about 5 minutes.
That means I have spent perhaps an hour on it  ten years.  Maybe
someday I will write the thing. I figure about three hours by the time I
set up the libraries and make files and fiddle for a bit. On the other
hand, I will probably retire within twenty years.  I am fifty, after all.
And, just maybe, it would take five minutes a year to use the programs.

assembler code from Fujitsu?  *That* would solve a lot of problems.
A GUI Life game would be a neat exercise.  Make it a dynamic web
page, in Cobol.
```

```
Donald Tees wrote:
> 
> Funny, but I have never heard this viewpoint before.  I was always
> taught that you fix whatever is wrong.  Fix bad data, bad programs,
> bad programmers, just make the damned thing work so we can all
> go home and play music or whatever.

Donald, personally I don't like the idea of patching the data because
I know it is very DANGEROUS. I will be a famous guy if my patching
program screw up the whole database and I have no doubt that even
the president will know my name too. Of course he will write me
a letter that saying "You are fired!" But in real life, I HAVE NO
CHOICE.

I work for a large corp with complicated data structure in huge
database. Theoretically, we can frozen the environment, work with
the database team and ask them to rollback the database to the
last backup version. Base on the audit trail system logs, re-run
all those online transactions and re-run any batch jobs until it
reaches the point where the job bombs. Fix the job and re-run
everything after it. Sound OK right?

In real terms it means to shut down all the user connections and
wait for the database team and the systems team fixing the problem.
It takes at least 30 working days and required more depends on
when the DB backup was.

If the company cannot wait for that period, then patching the
data is the only solution. Usually, it takes 3 to 5 days for the
patching including coding and testing.
 
> I will tell you something that is funny, but embarrassing.  I have my own
> Cobol system that I wrote myself to keep my own corporate books. I wrote
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> And, just maybe, it would take five minutes a year to use the programs.

Hey, it's normal. Not every work step need to be automate. That's why
we need to have the cost/benefit analysis report before the start of
every project. In the report, you need to show how much the company
can save if that particular job is automated. Only those project with
significant saving will be approved by the top management. Well for a
five minute work force saving within a year surely will be rejected 
by the management.

> BTW, noticed you in alt.asm86 the other day.  Have you called
> assembler code from Fujitsu?  *That* would solve a lot of problems.
> A GUI Life game would be a neat exercise.  Make it a dynamic web
> page, in Cobol.

No, nothing to do with COBOL. It's my son, who loves computer games,
now messing around VB using my old 486 PC. He needs a reboot routine
he can call from the VB so he can reboot the system and make those
newly modified autoexec.bat and config.sys file effective. You know
those games ask for a lot of convertional memory. I just send a 
mail for him and ask for the reboot routine.

Chip Ling
```

```

>Donald, personally I don't like the idea of patching the data because
>I know it is very DANGEROUS. I will be a famous guy if my patching
<Much snipped>

Well, that is true for sure, but on the other hand changing a working
program to handle one piece of bad data is just as dangerous.  I have
found bad data many times that is ... an anomaly? ... perhaps a bad track,
perhaps a power failure, perhaps a bug somewhere else.  But picture
a nice regular flat ASCII file with a big block of garbage in the middle.

If there is backup, great.  If there is not, then as you say, you have
no choice.  Also, going to backup in some circumstances creates
more problems than it solves.  Often, changing a program can
create greater havoc than fixing the data.  If you fix the data wrong,
there is probably a bad invoice out there. If you fix the program
wrong, there could be several thousand of them.

Mind you, I have an advantage.  Rather than working in a large Corp,
I work for myself, and the systems that I work on I wrote, so I know
what the data should look like and what effects it will have. My typical
installation is a dozen PC's in a network.

Interesting seeing the comments here.  I would bet that the risk
of changing data goes up in direct proportion to the system
complexity, and so the answer depends largely on what people
work with.
```

## Thread: [OT] Science *Proves* It! — 4 msgs

### [OT] Science *Proves* It!

- **From:** docdwarf@clark.net ()
- **Date:** 1998-06-23T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.software.year-2000,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6mohm8$ktn$1@clarknet.clark.net>`

```


From the letters to the editor, The Economist, June 20th 1998:


---begin quote
Wage inflation

SIR--Engineers and scientists
will never make as much money
as business executives. A rigorous
mathematical proof explains
why this is true:

Postulate 1: Knowledge is power
Postulate 2: Time is money

As every engineer knows:

           Power =     Work / Time

Since: Knowledge = Power
 and        Time = Money

Then: Knowledge =  Work / Money

Solving for money, we get:

    Money  = Work / Knowledge

    Thus, as knowledge approaches zero,
money approaches infinity
regardless of work done.

    Conclusion: The less you
know, the more you make (but
you probably knew that already).

Boston         PAUL WESEL

--end quote

DD
```

## Thread: Deperate student requires help with COBOL program — 4 msgs

_Topics: [`Tutorials, books, learning`](topics.md#learning)_

### Re: Deperate student requires help with COBOL program

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 1998-06-23T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6mq3hv$l6b@dfw-ixnews4.ix.netcom.com>`
- **References:** `<357864C7.54A9@virgin.net> <357AEF20.4D41@virgin.net> <357B38E3.8AF@nac.net> <-1306982101510001@a138009.san1.as.crl.com> <6m3ami$2rg_001@news.netdirect.net> <-1606981901340001@a138015.san1.as.crl.com> <35896D50.66C1@sympatico.ca> <6mf38s$qdt$7@heliodor.xara.net> <-2006981437020001@a138016.san1.as.crl.com> <6mmg8k$29c_004@news.netdirect.net> <-2306981914130001@a138012.san1.as.crl.com>`

```



Dennes wrote in message <-
   >much snippage<
>     so if i'm to learn properly, and trust
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>grow up and start doing the right thing. stop trying to be a hero.

As I stated in a much earlier note in this thread, it is entirely correct
for you to following your teacher's instructions when doing an assignment
(Just as you need to follow a user's requirements when creating application
logic in the "real world".)

HOWEVER, unless your instructor really has never written a real program
(which is possible, but I can hope not true), I think that if you approached
the instructor and asked "Is there ever an application design which makes it
correct to "correct" the data?" Then, I bet your teacher will admit there
are such times in the real world.  Let me repeat several techniques that are
each used based on application design requirements when you get bad data:
(There probably are more than this, but this will give you a good start -
that you could show your instructor and ask about.)

1) If some bad data is for a truly critical piece of information, then you
create an informative message (report, console, somewhere it will be seen)
with information to identify where the data came from, what is wrong with
it - and then you "gracefully" end the program.

2) If this is bad data in a field that fairly frequently gets incorrect
input (often in transactions requiring manual input), then you probably can
just create an error report (or transaction) and go on to the next record
(leaving a clear audit trail of what you skipped and why)

3) Let's say you are creating the actual online transaction where real
people enter data manually.  If they enter bad data, it is often possible to
make a guess as to what they really mean.  In such cases, you should go back
to the user with some type of message about "you entered "ABC", but this is
not valid - you probably mean "abc" - if so press enter to accept this,
otherwise, please re-enter the value for field XYZ"

4) Sometimes you really can guess what was meant by bad data.  A classic
example of this is when a numeric field is left justified instead of right
justified.  For example a 4 digit field (which accepts blank padding) had
"123 " instead of " 123".  If your REAL WORLD users tell you that this often
happens - even though it isn't supposed to and that this is OK, then you may
well FIX THE DATA to make it " 123" - write a report indicating that you did
this (and for which record).  Again, depending on the actual application
demands, it is possible that you will need to fix the data in the input file
before it goes to the next program in the application.  In other cases, you
may just fix the data in your temporary storage and keep the bad data in the
record.  THIS DEPENDS ENTIRELY on the application - what is needed and what
is expected.

  If you have an instructor who tells you that this type of thing will never
happen (and be what is best for the application), then YES, your instructor
is lying to you!  If on the other hand, your instructor says that your first
priority is to get your program to handle bad data in the application
defined way and then get whoever or whatever has created the bad data to fix
it, then the instructor is correct.

Nothing in the world will convince me that anyone who knows about any real
world computer application written since about 1965 would think that "crash
the system" is always the best way to handle bad data.  If you have an
instructor who really thinks that, please tell him/her that I am awfully
GLAD they are in the class room and not working at my Bank, Insurance
company, or even fast-food restaurant's data processing center.

Finally, if you are at a school that encourages to take EVERYTHING that any
instructor says as the ONLY way to think about it, then you in a military
organization (that never heard of Neurenburg and what following orders
means) and not at a school.  Use what your instructor tells you - but if you
don't understand WHY or how it fits in with the rest of the world (or even
why it seems to be different from what others are telling you) then you need
to ASK the teach to help you understand - and then you need to make up your
mind for yourself.
```

```

On Wed, 24 Jun 1998 05:39:20, "William M. Klein" 
<wmklein@ix.netcom.com> wrote:


> 
>   If you have an instructor who tells you that this type of thing will never
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> company, or even fast-food restaurant's data processing center.


Hey William, do you think a real world example would help.

We run a process at the end of the month that reads about 10 million 
records that come from 1000 different sources.  These records have the
julian DAY, no year, in them that must be converted to a gregorian 
date for a database load.

I generate a year, based on the current year and the current julian 
date, and use the function integer-of-day and then function 
date-of-integer to get the gregorian date.  

I'm sure you are aware that the intrinsic functions are intolerant of 
bad data.  Talk about programming to crash!  

Anyway, I see dates > 366, I see dates that are like '12 ' and '1  '. 
Any manor of editing.  I see dates with ' b ' and other letters in 
them!  

So I fix the data.  Numbers get deedited and anything that remains 
alpha gets a "boilerplate" date of 01-01-50 thrown in.  (Yes it's 2 
digit years, we don't need 4 digits to ID the record!).

Telling the people to fix their data is impossible.  It has to run, 
and there is a LEGAL requirement for this data, that it has to run 
NOW.  There are a lot of reasons to fix the data - and almost NONE for
blowing up (there are some though - ie NO data <G>).
```

- **From:** Chip Ling <chipling@sympatico.ca>
- **Date:** 1998-06-24T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35917F2A.5F88@sympatico.ca>`
- **References:** `<357864C7.54A9@virgin.net> <357AEF20.4D41@virgin.net> <357B38E3.8AF@nac.net> <-1306982101510001@a138009.san1.as.crl.com> <6m3ami$2rg_001@news.netdirect.net> <-1606981901340001@a138015.san1.as.crl.com> <35896D50.66C1@sympatico.ca> <6mf38s$qdt$7@heliodor.xara.net> <-2006981437020001@a138016.san1.as.crl.com> <6mmg8k$29c_004@news.netdirect.net> <-2306981914130001@a138012.san1.as.crl.com> <6mq3hv$l6b@dfw-ixnews4.ix.netcom.com> <Jl0PnHJ5PvPd-pn2-DMLd6Ovp50n8@Dwight_Miller.iix.com>`

```

Thane Hubbell wrote:
> 
> On Wed, 24 Jun 1998 05:39:20, "William M. Klein"
…[42 more quoted lines elided]…
> blowing up (there are some though - ie NO data <G>).

Deadline always play an important role on how we react to emergency.

I've worked for a Telco that also receive millions of toll calls
records from other carriers telco, they do it in a more formal
way. The frontend validation jobs will validate the incoming
data and then divides the data into two group, the good one
upload to the validated DB and the bad one upload to the
recycle DB. Of course the routine exception reports are a must.

The design of the validation jobs won't abent even with bad
data is welcome by the programmers and the operators. They
don't need to support the abend in the middle of the night.
The batch window is 10pm - 7am.

Then the users are required to handle all those exception data
in the recyle DB thru a small online system. They can either add/
delete/modify the data records. And they have 24 hours to work
on that before the monthly billing jobs start.

When the monthly billing jobs start, it will rerun the same
frontend validation job again but will feed in the records from
the recycle DB. If it still gets rejected, sorry you miss the
bus, and need to be stayed at the recycle DB until next month
billing.

But the idea still the same. "Edit the data" but in a more 
traceable way.

Rgds,
Chip Ling
```

```
> 
> Hey William, do you think a real world example would help.
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
> blowing up (there are some though - ie NO data <G>).

Thane, thanks for a very good real world example. Let me toss in my
$.02. My application is security movement & control (i.e., stocks,
bonds, and the like). Our batch cycle starts approx. 7:30 pm and runs
until 2:00 - 3:00 am. We have several external windows (e.g., the
Fed/SEC) which we either make or we pay a penalty (if we miss badly -
like a day (the whole system crashed and took a day to reconstruct) the
CEO gets a trip to DC to explain why). We also have several internal
windows which involve handing off files to other bank systems (trust
accounting, DDA, etc.). If we're late, they're late. The whole thing is
deeply intertwingled. The full day's outage happened before I arrived on
the scene, but I've been told that the programmers involved decided that
the data didn't need validating - save some time & run it. With systems
like these, if you're down when the markets open, you're not open for
business. (imagine many :-('s)

As far as the student & his/her bad data, has anyone suggested /
considered just asking the teacher, "What should I do if I find bad
data"?

Bill Lynch
```

- **From:** "Cobol Frog (Huib Klink)" <H.Klink@IMN.nl>
- **Date:** 1998-06-23T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35900AF4.8330B1FC@IMN.nl>`
- **References:** `<3584a349.24105251@news.gatecom.com> <35850C1C.FEC33F09@IMN.nl> <35851f0e$1$tfs$mr2ice@news> <6m3b1r$2rg_003@news.netdirect.net> <358535bc$2$tfs$mr2ice@news> <3586283A.B7921FA3@IMN.nl> <6m5a06$r65@sjx-ixn3.ix.netcom.com> <6mcr1l$7qm@bgtnsc02.worldnet.att.net> <358A84BF.7A84170C@IMN.nl> <u9xi1.3124$Iy.2859713@news4.atl.bellsouth.net> <358E2194.96A97226@IMN.nl> <Pine.OSF.3.95q.980622085107.21132A-100000@octane.dasb.fhda.edu>`

```
Ibis redibis numquam peribis wrote:

> Hence:
>       radar must always be  RADAR
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> I do not think so.

BTW, anybody knowing what "Ibis redibis numquam peribis" means?
```

```
On Tue, 23 Jun 1998, Cobol Frog (Huib Klink) wrote:

> Ibis redibis numquam peribis wrote:
> 
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
picked it up from the May issue of Culture Wars:

ibis       You shall go

redibis    You shall return

numquam    never

peribis    You shall perish

Idiomatically: a.) You shall go, you shall return, never shall you perish.
or             b.) You shall go, you shall return never, you shall perish.

Apparently Roman oracles hedged their bets.


In hindsight, after my forays into this NG, that handle seems much too
appropriate!

rrg

"Not as good as I used to be. Never was."
"Time costs money; even worse, money costs time."
```

- **From:** "Donald Tees" <donald@willmack.com>
- **Date:** 1998-06-23T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6mpgam$28h$1@news.igs.net>`
- **References:** `<3584a349.24105251@news.gatecom.com> <35850C1C.FEC33F09@IMN.nl> <35851f0e$1$tfs$mr2ice@news> <6m3b1r$2rg_003@news.netdirect.net> <358535bc$2$tfs$mr2ice@news> <3586283A.B7921FA3@IMN.nl> <6m5a06$r65@sjx-ixn3.ix.netcom.com> <6mcr1l$7qm@bgtnsc02.worldnet.att.net> <358A84BF.7A84170C@IMN.nl> <u9xi1.3124$Iy.2859713@news4.atl.bellsouth.net> <358E2194.96A97226@IMN.nl> <Pine.OSF.3.95q.980622085107.21132A-100000@octane.dasb.fhda.edu> <0nNj1.525$w05.757416@news1.atl.bellsouth.net>`

```

.  >This is not so with COBOL, FORTRAN or APL.

Well now, if we are going to get picky, Cobol and APL are
quite different cases.  If we called it Apple, then it would be
a name.  A name is a spoken word, as is Cobol.  How a
name is derived is irrelevant as to the fact that it is a name.
Would you insist that Smith be spelt lower case because it
is a standard noun?  Based on standard usage, capitalisation
depends upon usage, to an extent.  It would be correct, for example,
to say that Fujitsu Cobol is a compiler for the COBOL language.

'course, it only matters if you are writing in c.
donald
```

```
Newsgroups: comp.lang.cobol
Subject: Re: Is there still good Cobal career after year 2000?

.  >This is not so with COBOL, FORTRAN or APL.

Well now, if we are going to get picky, Cobol and APL are
quite different cases.  If we called it Apple, then it would be
a name.  A name is a spoken word, as is Cobol.  How a
name is derived is irrelevant as to the fact that it is a name.
Would you insist that Smith be spelt lower case because it
is a standard noun?  Based on standard usage, capitalisation
depends upon usage, to an extent.  It would be correct, for example,
to say that Fujitsu Cobol is a compiler for the COBOL language.

'course, it only matters if you are writing in c.
donald
```

```


From donald@willmack.com Tue Jun 23 23:24:47 1998
Date: Tue, 23 Jun 1998 21:12:31 -0400
From: Donald Tees <donald@willmack.com>
Newsgroups: comp.lang.cobol
Subject: Re: Is there still good Cobal career after year 2000?

P.S. to that last.  Having gone and checked my Fowlers, Chicago Manual
of Style, Canadian Press Handbook, etc., I am informed that the correct
usage in English is as follows:

An acronym, when used as a name, is capitalised normally if spoken as a
word.  If spoken by repeating the individual letters, then all letters are
capitalised.

Thus:   Cobol, Fortran, APL, and PL1.
```

```

(Doug Miller) wrote:
> 
> In article <358BB546.98C74350@zip.com.au>,
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> things down too much? Sorry, but this just doesn't make sense.

Thinks....  er um you are right.  If I am doing the check why can't I do
the check cleanly.

In my defense a simple abend routine adds far less overhead on code than
trying to continue when things do screw up.  I have seen a deep looping
program that tried to recover from a problem and every second statement
ended up IF NOT ERROR multiply this but millions of transactions and...
It does come down to the 90/10 rule.

Sometimes the simple solution is the simple solution.  Use you own
training to determine when and where to apply the generic rules and when
to break them.

The final point is even with a cleanly defined interface things can and
do go wrong. For example unlabelled tapes coming from offsite get
shuffled and cause havoc in production programs that have been running
for years.
```

#### ↳ Re: Here is one person view, what do you think?

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc123@bellsouth.net>
- **Date:** 1998-06-24T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4_ak1.1056$w05.1600699@news1.atl.bellsouth.net>`
- **References:** `<357C0243.F91ED7EF@epamail.epa.gov> <Jl0PnHJ5PvPd-pn2-qQWNwdRUzXgl@Dwight_Miller.iix.com> <6lh5mu$fnr$1@websites.campbellhall.org> <6li8sb$6vd@bgtnsc03.worldnet.att.net> <3584CAE0.27FE4D4C@cpgen.cpg.com.au> <Jl0PnHJ5PvPd-pn2-ZLZRoTLZGtE2@Dwight_Miller.iix.com> <35912dbc.0@news.proweb.co.uk>`

```

Steve wrote:
>
>I'm a relative beginner at programming especially in the windows environment
>so excuse the question, but what is a sliding window. I've not come across
>this term before.


Let me give you a real example which I coded just last week for a client.
Assume that the 2 digit year is within the century beginning 79 years ago
and ending 20 years in the future.  You could make any other assumption you
like about the target century, so long as the span is 100 years, but this was
appropriate for our data.  You can easily resolve (interpret) the date to
be within that period by using the computer date and a little logic, like
this:

       01  DATE-WORK-AREA.
           03  DW-TODAYS-CCYY    PIC  9(04).
           03  DW-TODAYS-CCYY-R  REDEFINES DW-TODAYS-CCYY.
               05  DW-TODAYS-CC      PIC  9(02).
               05  DW-TODAYS-DD      PIC  9(02).

           03  DW-CCYY           PIC  9(04).
           03  DW-CCYY-R         REDEFINES DW-CCYY.
               05  DW-CC             PIC  9(02).
               05  DW-YY             PIC  9(02).

Populate DW-TODAYS-CCYY from computer date at program BOJ.  Then, when
you get ready to 'window' a 2 digit year, do this:

       MOVE DW-TODAYS-CC   TO DW-CC.
       MOVE <2 digit year> TO DW-YY.
       IF (DW-CCYY < DW-TODAYS-CCYY - 79)
           ADD 100 TO DW-CCYY
       ELSE
           IF (DW-CCYY > DW-TODAYS-CCYY + 20)
               SUBTRACT 100 FROM DW-CCYY.

Note that if the actual date is February 29, and DW-YY = zero, you will
need to examine DW-CC to see if it is a leap year.  If DW-CC MOD 4 = 0,
then the century year is a leap year, otherwise not.  If you don't take
this step, February 29, 2100, and 2200, 2300, 2500, etc., will be accepted
as valid, although 2100, 2200, 2300, 2500, etc. will not be leap years. ;-)

Because this method assumes the input date falls within a specified
'window', relative to the current date, you never have a problem as time
progresses, because the computer date progresses.  Dates such as birth
dates usually cannot be resolved this way, because in early 2000, there
will be birth dates spanning three centuries (18xx, 19xx, 20xx), even
for living people.  Only if you can assume that the dates will always
fall with some window <= 100 years, relative to some known date, such as
computer date, can this method be used.

Windowing is very useful to permit users to continue entering 6 digit
dates indefinitely, for transaction dates or other dates which will
always fall within the moving window.
```

- **From:** john@watkins.cix.co.uk (John Watkins)
- **Date:** 1998-06-24T00:00:00

```

In article <35912dbc.0@news.proweb.co.uk>, sfoster@proweb.co.uk (Steve)
wrote:

> Thane Hubbell wrote in message ...
> >On Mon, 15 Jun 1998 07:18:56, Esmond Pitt <pitte@cpgen.cpg.com.au>
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> this term before.
 
It's nothing to do with 'Windows', but is a way of implementing conversion
from two digit years to four digit years.

We use a window of -30 to +70 from the current date, so at the moment:

              Now
  |------------X---------------------------|
1968          1998                       2067

Any date entered with values greater than 68 uses the lower date century,
and any date less than 68 uses the upper date century.


+--------------+------------------------------------+
| John Watkins | mailto:john@watkins_dot_cix.co.uk  |
|              | http://www_dot_cix.co.uk/~watkins/ |
+--------------+------------------------------------+
```

```
"Steve" <sfoster@proweb.co.uk> wrote:

…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Steve:

Uh Oh...Now Thane is going to ask us to support "sliding GUI windows"
in sp2.  I guess the function parameter would be something like
SP2-CONVERSE-GREASED-PANEL.

Thane wasn't referring to a "GUI" windows when he was speaking of
"sliding windows" he was referring to a technique which is used in
some Year 2000 remediation projects.

The "sliding window" he was referring to was a "window of time" which
allows the application to correctly determine whether the 2 digit year
in question is attributable to the 20th century (i.e. "19") or the
21st century (i.e. "20")

The "sliding" part refers to the ability for the window of time to
periodically adjust so that the application can continue to correctly
determine the proper century digits.

How'd I do, Thane??

I will refrain from expressing my opinion on whether or not windowing
techniques are good or bad because it will start a thread which won't
die until we all start worrying about the Y10K problem....besides, I
am an amatuer when it comes to discussing Y2K solutions.

I prefer to express my opinions on GUI windowing issues.

Hope that helps.



Bob Wolfe, flexus, rtwolfe at spammers-are-meat-heads flexus dot com
To reply, look at my e-mail address, get rid of the spammer insult and fix the address.
Check out The Flexus COBOL Page at http://www.flexus.com
```

```
Here's the drill, John. You can contract with :Dave, Infomagic, Shmuel,
my pal Rex  "the software archeologist" ... there are about a half dozen
other tuff-guys that I'm aware of on c.s.y2k,  please don't bother 
asking for a guarentee, and don't dicker on price, we don't have time to
discuss price.

For a job like this, you really want a tuff-guy.  If there's a lot of 
source, you want a team of pro's.  You don't want to dance with one of 
the low cost Y2K remediation companies.

Here're a few questions for you:

1) Do you have a working compiler and development environment?

2) Are we talking batch COBOL or something extra like IMS, CICS, etc?

3) In general what does the program/system do?

4) What operating system and what level?

5) Can we telnet in?

6) Anyone around who worked on the source?

7) Do you want us to Y2K the code too or is producing the source 
adequate?

8) How much time do we have?  I'm guessing that this is a Y2K job.  If 
so, is December 1998 too late?

9) Is Assembler source acceptable or are you stuck on COBOL?

10) If you're stuck on COBOL, does the job include rolling the source
ahead to a current compiler/environment?

Any of us can do the job.  Depending on the amount of source and our 
schedule, we may decline to take the assignment.

cory hamasaki  554 days...
```

```
sound.

best tools available it is only a nearly imposible task.
```

## Thread: Problem with cobol cics program calling assembler module — 10 msgs

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe)_

### Re: Problem with cobol cics program calling assembler module

- **From:** Bal Oberoi <oberoi@ibm.net>
- **Date:** 1998-06-24T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35910C96.5082@ibm.net>`
- **References:** `<01bd9dbe$09ffd470$3303a8c0@pcqu>`

```

Erich Quant wrote:
 
> I have a big problem and hope you could give me a hint: We have CICS
> COBOL/COBOL2 application system that is using several assembler 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> application.
 
I seem to remember reading somewhere that a called program in CICS 
environment should not have an alternate entry point, i.e. ENTRY point 
of the module should be same as the CSECT name. I have looked but have 
not yet located where I read this; can anyone else confirm this?

Another thing to check:  Your ASSTSTP module is set to use AMODE=31 
and RMODE=ANY.  What about the calling program? 
"If you are passing control between programs executing in different 
addressing modes, you must change the AMODE indicator in the PSW". 
(MVS Assembler Services Reference, GC28-1910).

Module ASSTSTP does not issue any CICS commands, so it does not 
have to be processed by CICS Translator, nor do you need an entry 
for it in the PPT.  That being so, all CALLs to ASSTSTP *have* to 
be static; neither dynamic CALLs nor a CICS LINK is possible.

Hope this is of some help!
MfG, 
Bal
```

#### ↳ Re: Problem with cobol cics program calling assembler module

- **From:** daniel.jacot@winterthur.ch
- **Date:** 1998-06-25T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6mt51o$f41$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com>`
- **References:** `<01bd9dbe$09ffd470$3303a8c0@pcqu> <35910C96.5082@ibm.net>`

```

In article <35910C96.5082@ibm.net>,
  oberoi@ibm.net wrote:
>
> Erich Quant wrote:
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I have never tried to use an alternate entry in such a program, but I can't
imagine that this should cause any problem at all.

> Another thing to check:  Your ASSTSTP module is set to use AMODE=31
> and RMODE=ANY.  What about the calling program?

Another question which seems much more important: Is your assembler-module
linked reentrant? And is it really reentrant?

> "If you are passing control between programs executing in different
> addressing modes, you must change the AMODE indicator in the PSW".
> (MVS Assembler Services Reference, GC28-1910).
>

The dynamic COBOL-call does this automatically for you!

> Module ASSTSTP does not issue any CICS commands, so it does not
> have to be processed by CICS Translator, nor do you need an entry
> for it in the PPT.  That being so, all CALLs to ASSTSTP *have* to
> be static; neither dynamic CALLs nor a CICS LINK is possible.
>

I am shure that you have to define your ASSEMBLER in the PPT when called
dynamically from COBOL. And via COBOL, you don't load the module from the
STEPLIB but rather from the DFHRPL. Only ASSEMBLER-ASSEMBLER via the LINK- or
LOAD-macro works directly without CICS between (and with poor performance for
the whole CICS)! Both dynamic calls and CICS LINK work fine. I wrote a little
ASSEMBLER which is used in production via dynamic call since one year and I
use CICS LINK with several different ASSEMBLER routines.

> Hope this is of some help!
> MfG,
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Cheers

Daniel


"Murpy was an optimist!"

http://www.dejanews.com/   Now offering spam-free web-based newsreading
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Problem with cobol cics program calling assembler module

- **From:** Bal Oberoi <oberoi@ibm.net>
- **Date:** 1998-06-26T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3593AA30.704E@ibm.net>`
- **References:** `<01bd9dbe$09ffd470$3303a8c0@pcqu> <35910C96.5082@ibm.net> <6mt51o$f41$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com>`

```

daniel.jacot@winterthur.ch wrote:


> 
> > "If you are passing control between programs executing in different
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> The dynamic COBOL-call does this automatically for you!

Agreed. However, unless Erich has the time and inclination to make 
changes to his Assembler programs, dynamic CALLs are not an option.
For a program to be called dynamically it has to satisfy several 
requirements.  A couple I can think of are:
1. It must be CICS-aware, i.e. it must be coded to expect 
   EIB and CommArea as the first two among the parameters that the 
   calling program passes to it.
2. The subprogram should be passed through the Translator prior to 
   assembly, and it should have an entry in the PPT. 

> 
> > Another thing to check:  Your ASSTSTP module is set to use 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> assembler-module linked reentrant? And is it really reentrant?

If the CALL to the subprogram is static (i.e. it is linkedited as 
part of *each* calling COBOL load module) then reentrancy is not a 
requirement; it's enough if it's serially reuseable, or 
"quasi-reentrant". 

Regards,
Bal
----- 
oberoi@ibm.net
http://www.geocities.com/~oberoi/mainfrme.html
-----
"The universe is full of magical things, 
patiently waiting for our wits to grow sharper." - Eden Phillpotts
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Problem with cobol cics program calling assembler module

- **From:** Frank Swarbrick <infocat@sprynet.com>
- **Date:** 1998-06-26T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35945209.79B9@sprynet.com>`
- **References:** `<01bd9dbe$09ffd470$3303a8c0@pcqu> <35910C96.5082@ibm.net> <6mt51o$f41$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com> <3593AA30.704E@ibm.net>`

```

Bal Oberoi wrote:
> 
> > unknown person wrote:
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
>    assembly, and it should have an entry in the PPT.

I believe this is not true.  I have a completely batch program called CHKALPHA.PHASE.
As long as it is in the PPT, I can call it the following way from a CICS program:

working-storage section.
  01  CHKALPHA    PIC X(8)   VALUE 'CHKALPHA'.

procedure division.
  CALL CHKALPHA USING [parameters].

Because I am calling it, not LINKing to it, the EIB and COMMAREAs are not
required in the called program.

Now, CHKALPHA is a COBOL II program, not an assembler program, but I can't
imagine why assembler programs could not be called the same way.

Note that we use VSE, not MVS, but I doubt this makes a difference.
```

```
>Bal Oberoi wrote:
>> 
…[37 more quoted lines elided]…
>work: frank.swarbrick@1stbank.com

You are correct.  This is a static call.  The object of CHKALPHA is
embedded in the object of the calling program at linkedit time and,
therefore, doesn't need a PPT entry nor needs to be aware of the EIB
and COMMAREA.  However, if the program was called using EXEC CICS LINK
or EXEC CICS XCTL, it would have to be CICS eligible.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Problem with cobol cics program calling assembler module

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** Bill Lynch <wblynch@worldnet.att.net>
- **Date:** 1998-06-27T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6n41at$5lg@bgtnsc03.worldnet.att.net>`
- **References:** `<01bd9dbe$09ffd470$3303a8c0@pcqu> <35910C96.5082@ibm.net> <6mt51o$f41$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com> <3593AA30.704E@ibm.net> <35945209.79B9@sprynet.com> <359523ca.3949052@news.apk.net>`

```

SkippyPB wrote:
> 
(snip)
> >I believe this is not true.  I have a completely batch program called CHKALPHA.PHASE.
> >As long as it is in the PPT, I can call it the following way from a CICS program:
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> or EXEC CICS XCTL, it would have to be CICS eligible.

No, no, calling a data_name is *always* a dynamic call, even with the
NODYNAM compiler option. If you're calling an Asm pgm and it doesn't
issue CICS commands, you needn't pass the EIB & Commarea; but you
*always* need a PPT entry for a dynamic call (that's inherent in it's
being dynamic). In the above case the called module (CHKALPHA) will not
be made part of the load module by the linkage editor/Binder.

I think you're mixing the notions of dynamic vs. static with the ability
to issue CICS commands or not.

Bill Lynch

(snipped)
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Problem with cobol cics program calling assembler module

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Problem with cobol cics program calling assembler module

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** Bal Oberoi <oberoi@ibm.net>
- **Date:** 1998-06-27T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3595300F.66C2@ibm.net>`
- **References:** `<01bd9dbe$09ffd470$3303a8c0@pcqu> <35910C96.5082@ibm.net> <6mt51o$f41$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com> <3593AA30.704E@ibm.net> <35945209.79B9@sprynet.com>`

```

Frank Swarbrick wrote:

> > > > "If you are passing control between programs executing in 
> > > > different addressing modes, you must change the AMODE indicator 
> > > > in the PSW".  (MVS Assembler Services Reference, GC28-1910).

> > > The dynamic COBOL-call does this automatically for you!

> >
> > Agreed. However, unless Erich has the time and inclination to make
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> >    assembly, and it should have an entry in the PPT.

> 
> I believe this is not true.  I have a completely batch program called 
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> Note that we use VSE, not MVS, but I doubt this makes a difference.

Well, I stand corrected! I have never seen it done, but the manual, 
(COBOL for MVS/VM, Prog. Gde.), certainly backs you up:

* CALL identifier can be used with the NODYNAM compiler option to 
  dynamically call a program.  Called programs can contain any 
  function supported by CICS for the language.  Dynamically called 
  programs have to be defined in the CICS program processing table 
  (PPT).
* If you are calling a COBOL program that has been translated, you 
  must pass DFHEIBLK and DFHCOMMAREA as the first two parameters in 
  the CALL statement.

It's a tad ambiguous. But based on your experience it would appear 
that: 
  If a subprogram, called dynamically, does not use any CICS 
  functions, it does not have to be translated. 
  And if the Translator did not stick any CICS-related stuff into the 
  code, it does not have to be passed pointers to EIB and CommArea.
  In any event, for a dynamic CALL to work, the called program must 
  have an entry in the PPT.

Thanks for sharing your experience, Frank.  

Best of regards,
Bal
----- 
oberoi@ibm.net
http://www.geocities.com/~oberoi/mainfrme.html
-----
"The universe is full of magical things, 
patiently waiting for our wits to grow sharper." - Eden Phillpotts
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6mq90p$gjk$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com>`
- **References:** `<357864C7.54A9@virgin.net> <357AEF20.4D41@virgin.net> <357B38E3.8AF@nac.net> <-1306982101510001@a138009.san1.as.crl.com> <6m3ami$2rg_001@news.netdirect.net> <-1606981901340001@a138015.san1.as.crl.com> <35896D50.66C1@sympatico.ca> <6mf38s$qdt$7@heliodor.xara.net> <-2006981437020001@a138016.san1.as.crl.com> <6mmg8k$29c_004@news.netdirect.net> <-2306981914130001@a138012.san1.as.crl.com>`

```

I've been following this thread since it started and as someone learning to
program in COBOL i've found the different view points "interesting. I'm
currently working as an operator and as an end user i wish more programmers
would take the time to fix bad data or at least stop  programs crashing when
they get any. Quick theoritcal example say you are running a program that
takes information about orders placed on your company and turns them into
despatch notes / invoices etc. if the program just crashed if the first set
of data was invalid then you wouldnt be able to get the rest of the
information until the problem is fixed even though the next 1000+ sets of
data may be completley valid. It just dosen't make sense to just stop the
program. ignore the data, store it else where whatever but don't just crash
out the program after all its going to be easier to reprocess one set of data
than 1000. Anyway thats just my opinion

-----== Posted via Deja News, The Leader in Internet Discussion ==-----
http://www.dejanews.com/   Now offering spam-free web-based newsreading
```

#### ↳ Re: Deperate student requires help with COBOL program

- **From:** mark0young@aol.com (Mark0Young)
- **Date:** 1998-06-25T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1998062500015900.UAA22514@ladder01.news.aol.com>`
- **References:** `<6mq90p$gjk$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com>`

```

In article <6mq90p$gjk$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com>, LOGANAJ_ALEX@my-dejanews.com
writes:

>i wish more programmers
>would take the time to fix bad data or at least stop  programs crashing when
>they get any.

At work, our philosophy is pretty much "let the program do what it can, report
what it can't, but abend only in the most dire situation." Bad data should
never cause an abend. However, recognizing the wrong input file or having the
master data base unavailable could be valid reasons for abending the job
(batch) or rejecting data (CICS) _after_ an appropriate message is displayed
somewhere where Operations (batch) or the terminal user (CICS) can see what is
clearly wrong.

But in no case should bad raw (unedited) data cause any program to abend.
(Well, almost no. If this is a critical control date, it may be worth causing
an abend after printing an appropriate message to let the operator know that
something has to be fixed before other jobs can be run.)

There is a grey area, though: computer-generated data received from another
site that should be perfectly good is sometimes handled without editing
routines. However, I think a certain amount of data validation should still go
on because we have seen abends caused downstream when an upstream editing
routine could have caught the problems.

And in absolutely no case should a programmer code an abend without producing
an informative message first.

Mark A. Young
```

---

## Thread: Open a Db in a called program — 6 msgs

### Open a Db in a called program

- **From:** info@primultini.it (UZ)

```

Hello all,

how can I do that the file opened in the PRG1 can be used also in the
PRG 2 in the case that the PRG 1 calls the PRG 2, without reopen it ??
Now I must open and close it in the called program, while it would be
very good if I could define it as "external file" in the called
program.
There's some declaration in the FD  section ??

p.s.: i'm  using MF cobol 3.2

Thanks in advance,

bye. 
Ugo - Italy
e-mail: info@primultini.it
```

```
Use the EXTERNAL attribute in the FD.
```

```
I think it also chews up memory.
That's my $0.02 worth
```

#### ↳ Re: Open a Db in a called program

- **From:** "Leif Svalgaard" <leif@ibm.net>
- **Date:** 1998-06-25T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<359291de.0@news1.ibm.net>`
- **References:** `<6mqfn6$s3c$3@server-b.cs.interbusiness.it>`

```

>
>how can I do that the file opened in the PRG1 can be used also in the
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>

One way is to have a THIRD program (FILEIO) which has the FD and I/O
section and all that. Thus take the I/O out of PRG1 and PRG2. Then
call FILEIO to do the I/O. You need to set up a linkage section with
1) some control information (do you want open/close/read/write, etc)
2) the record layout.
```

```
Francis, A., Ney, Jr wrote:
> 
> > From: The Goobers <docdwarf@erols.com>
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> manager's permanent employment record, with attendant consequences.

Why of *course* that's it... silly me, I thought a team was made of of
people with differing skills, lead by a 'coach'; what it *really* is
must be a group of completely anonymous and interchangeable units, none
of which 'makes waves'.

> 
> What makes you think a PHM is going to put his crank on the chopping block for
> a bunch of ungrateful pencil-necked geeks?  It works both ways, too.
> Subordinates to PHMs like this end up doing the minimum necessary to stay
> employed.

Now it is time for... A Story!  Years ago I was watching a TV show about
the siege of Stalingrad; one of the fellows they were interviewing was a
Red Army veteran who was reminiscing about the Good Old Days. ('We khad,
forrr feefteen maan, wan po-tay-to forrr thrrreeee veeks... it vas so
cold dat yourrr peess vould frreeeeze beforrre it khit da ground...
ahhhhh, good old days!') Anyhow, he told a tale about Field Marshall
Zhukov, who had started as a cavalry sergeant in the Czar's army...
Zhukov was being driven from the front back to HQ when he saw two
injured soldiers hitch-hiking back to the hospital tent (funny how they
keep that tent with the big Red Cross right next to the HQ tent, neh?)

Zhukov stopped his driver and asked the soldiers how long they had been
there; they told him an hour, maybe an hour and a half... he said 'Wait
a while longer, it will be worth it'... and had his driver continue down
the road, around the bend, where he then stopped the car.

Every car which drove past was also stopped; when he did not see the
injured soldiers Zhukov ordered the officers out of their vehicle and
sent their drivers on... in a half-hour or so he had quite a crowd
around him, maybe 20 - 30 officers, and the car he stopped contained the
soldiers.  In front of these enlisted men Zhukov then publically reamed
his own officers, demoted each of them two grades in rank and sent them
*back* to the front with the admonition 'YOU DO NOT TREAT MY MEN IN THIS
WAY'.

The reminiscer then said 'The next day this story was over *all* the
armies...' ... and I remember thinking, Sergeant that I was at that time
(weekender, AFRES), 'Hot *damn*... I would march into hell for such a
man.'

The troops eat first, *then* the officers... maybe this is one of the
reasons I never went into Management.

> 
> Now do you see why I think Y2K is going to be a complete tits-up event?  Our
> dear leaders aren't leading, and they're sure as hell not supporting the
> people they're supposed to be leading (and who end up doing the real work).

SNAFU... TARFU... FUBAR, this is not news.

DD
```

### Re: history of cobol

- **From:** vbandke@bsp-gmbh.com (Volker Bandke)
- **Date:** 1998-06-24T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<F8fMbxuvNgmM-pn2-BPDVy0IGv6Fh@localhost>`
